        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1604040945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12412343684021452115">Barchester Towers is second in the series of The Barsetshire Chronicles by Anthony Trollope, a series of six novels, set in the fictitious English county of Barsetshire and its cathedral town of Barchester. <br/><br/>The position of Warden at Hiram’s Hospital is still unoccupied at the beginning of the novel. The Bishop of Barchester is on his deathbed. Several people have their eyes on the Bishop’s chair including Mr. Harding’s son-in-law and the Archdeacon, Dr. Grantly. However, much to the dismay of ma</span>
  <span id="freeText12412343684021452115" style="display:none">Barchester Towers is second in the series of The Barsetshire Chronicles by Anthony Trollope, a series of six novels, set in the fictitious English county of Barsetshire and its cathedral town of Barchester. <br /><br />The position of Warden at Hiram’s Hospital is still unoccupied at the beginning of the novel. The Bishop of Barchester is on his deathbed. Several people have their eyes on the Bishop’s chair including Mr. Harding’s son-in-law and the Archdeacon, Dr. Grantly. However, much to the dismay of many, Dr. Proudie is chosen to continue as the Bishop with a wife who tries to meddle in his decisions. Mrs. Proudie is all ears for the advices of Dr. Proudie’s detestable chaplain, Mr. Slope, who has several plans up his sleeve, including the chair of Dean. When Dr. Stanhope takes up the duties of the parish, he brings with him an interesting array of family members including his married, crippled, seductive daughter who goes by the name, Signora and his debt ridden son, Bertie, who is looking for a good alliance for financial stability. Eleanor, now widowed (yes, that was shocking especially since I expected to see the married life of Eleanor and Mr. Bold in this novel) is central to the distinct romantic storyline in the novel. She has three suitors (unknown to her)- Mr. Slope, Bertie and Dr. Arabin.<br /><br />The character sketch is brilliant. Slope is obnoxious and reminds me of Uriah Heep in David Copperfield. The novel has many funny and comical moments, hence it is of a lighter nature than that of its prequel, The Warden. I enjoyed it more than The Warden.<br /><br />Read a full review here - <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.thebooksatchel.com/barchester-towers-by-anthony-trollope/">http://www.thebooksatchel.com/barches...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12412343684021452115" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1669404273" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1669404273" />
    <a title="classic reverie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37374526-classic-reverie"><img alt="classic reverie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480288607p2/37374526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1669404273?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="classic reverie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="classic reverie" href="/user/show/37374526-classic-reverie">classic reverie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141199115" href="/book/show/14800670-barchester-towers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=reading-focus-2018">reading-focus-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=barsetshire-series">barsetshire-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=trollope">trollope</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=english-writer">english-writer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=part-of-a-series">part-of-a-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=poltical-bend">poltical-bend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=1800">1800</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=religious-element-predominance">religious-element-predominance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1669404273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4116398708432193429">Can you read Anthony Trollope's "Barchester Towers" without reading his first in this series "The Warden" first? Yes!!! Trollope does a great job at setting the scene when it is needed so you can understand his second in the series better, but why would you miss out on "all that". I am reading my way through these six stories this year and I am glad I am going in order. That being said, new characters arrive and some leave which makes this even more delightful. Since I started reading the classi</span>
  <span id="freeText4116398708432193429" style="display:none">Can you read Anthony Trollope's "Barchester Towers" without reading his first in this series "The Warden" first? Yes!!! Trollope does a great job at setting the scene when it is needed so you can understand his second in the series better, but why would you miss out on "all that". I am reading my way through these six stories this year and I am glad I am going in order. That being said, new characters arrive and some leave which makes this even more delightful. Since I started reading the classic about 8 years ago, I have always found the religious element in these stories interesting for many reasons and what I have read of Trollope, he does not disappoint the reader in this. For one reason you can see the changes that taking place in religious society &amp; the thoughts of authors and the general public at large. In "The Warden" and "Barchester Towers" we get a taste of church politics and the quest of both the modern and traditional faction in their fight for what they believe this institution should provide. Do you have to be a religious person to enjoy or appreciate? Heck no!! If it is about religion it has to be boring? Are you kidding me!!! I think a religious element brings so much of the human element to view in both good verses evil and the in between which humans are prone to be. What are the ingredients that make this a favorite of mine? Add a rivalry between factions for the Bishop postion; a sheep of a man that is ruled by either his tyrant wife or a grasping chaplain; a quadangle (similar to a triangle with regards lovers); a family of heartless rogues and a beautiful Italian senorita who spins webs from an invalid sofa, you have a soap opera of misunderstanding and mishaps which keep the reader interested until the very last paragraph, or at least it was for me! Looking forward to the next in the series to see how it all plays out. I read a Delphi collection of his work, which I have many notes and highlights, many public but no spoilers or at least I don't think so! :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4116398708432193429" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer371889917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5011508159121234540">I came into this book completely blind. I only wanted to read a book by Anthony Trollope, because I never had before, and I had heard that his Barsetshire Chronicles were considered his best. So voila, here I am. <br/><br/>So. First of all, I know diddly poop about the Anglican church. I do not know the difference between High Church and Low Church, or between a vicar and a dean or what a prebendary is. I know enough to get by reading Jane Austen, or to get a laugh of out of the Tarts & Vicars party in Br</span>
  <span id="freeText5011508159121234540" style="display:none">I came into this book completely blind. I only wanted to read a book by Anthony Trollope, because I never had before, and I had heard that his Barsetshire Chronicles were considered his best. So voila, here I am. <br /><br />So. First of all, I know diddly poop about the Anglican church. I do not know the difference between High Church and Low Church, or between a vicar and a dean or what a prebendary is. I know enough to get by reading Jane Austen, or to get a laugh of out of the Tarts &amp; Vicars party in Bridget Jones' Diary. Fine. So when I realized that the entire plot of this book revolves around a new bishop being appointed, and how he dispenses the various "livings" in his jurisdiction, I wasn't sure I should press on. But I got into it, at least enough to follow the action, and mostly because I needed to make sure that Mr. Slope "got his" and that poor Eleanor was all right. Poor Eleanor, a rich and beautiful widow, surrounded by idiots. Her father, bless his heart, is a big baby, and her brother-in-law is a total ass. I kind of hoped toward the end that she would tell him to get stuffed, but sadly that would not make her a virtuous Victorian heroine. *sigh*<br /><br />So, a very interesting book. Much more sly than I had thought at first appearance, though without the more abundant humor of, say, Mrs. Gaskell.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5011508159121234540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TbzU4rjRcvIrQ1sG0+v1V95BTP1CLhKB5ywGM868iVerKcO7molIF32B4SzsHeEtjsGy4epsK5xh/r7b0S96Nw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MuvWNGIZcSaZTLsouhNGhcr8ZDIGwCOC6qzB2V6b+MTUfsFtQEFLw8+OAQKF5VL/mnyaLq6CGp9sfnkxQQgLpA==')})
  }
//]]>

Her wry account of rural life is undercut, however, by tragedy in its depiction of such troubling events as Matty\'s bankruptcy, the violent death of Captain Brown or the unwitting cruelty of Peter Jenkyns. Unlike the &quot;New Woman&quot; novels of the era which challenged the idea that the unmarried woman was superfluous, Gissing satirizes that image and portrays women as &quot;odd&quot; and marginal in relation to an ideal. Set in a grimy, fog-ridden London, Gissing\'s &quot;odd&quot; women range from the idealistic, financially self-sufficient Mary Barfoot to the Madden sisters who struggle to subsist in low paying jobs and little chance for joy. With narrative detachment, Gissing portrays contemporary society\'s blatant ambivalence towards its own period of transition. Added to the stories are extracts from the novels in which the men of the law make their mark. These law officers and the circumstances in which they work were based on Dickens\' observations of the fledgling police detective force when he was a solicitor\'s clerk and reporter. He accompanied detectives on their nightly patrols of the streets of London, witnessed the day-to-day running of police stations, attended magistrates\' courts, and was present at murder trials and public executions. Out of these eyewitness experiences grew Mr. Nadgett in Martin Chuzzlewit—the first serious detective in an English novel—and Inspector Bucket in Bleak House, who solves the murder of an unscrupulous lawyer. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12683589394602299606">Anthony Trollope became one of the most successful, prolific and respected English novelists of the Victorian era. Some of Trollope's best-loved works, known as the Chronicles of Barsetshire, revolve around the imaginary county of Barsetshire; he also wrote penetrating novels on political, social, and gender issues and conflicts of his day.<br/><br/>Trollope has always been a popular novelist. Noted fans ha</span>
  <span id="freeText12683589394602299606" style="display:none">Anthony Trollope became one of the most successful, prolific and respected English novelists of the Victorian era. Some of Trollope's best-loved works, known as the Chronicles of Barsetshire, revolve around the imaginary county of Barsetshire; he also wrote penetrating novels on political, social, and gender issues and conflicts of his day.<br /><br />Trollope has always been a popular novelist. Noted fans have included Sir Alec Guinness (who never travelled without a Trollope novel), former British Prime Ministers Harold Macmillan and Sir John Major, economist John Kenneth Galbraith, American novelists Sue Grafton and Dominick Dunne and soap opera writer Harding Lemay. Trollope's literary reputation dipped somewhat during the last years of his life, but he regained the esteem of critics by the mid-twentieth century.<br />See also <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthony_Trollope">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthony_...</a><br /></span>
